<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Age of Discovery</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Period of European global exploration from the 15th century to the 18th century</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2827" data-file-width="2034" decoding="async" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg/250px-Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg/375px-Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg/500px-Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caravela_Vera_Cruz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Portuguese invention of the <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a>, which was maneuverable and able to undertake ocean voyages, was essential to European maritime exploration. The present image shows the "<i>Caravela Vera Cruz</i>", navigating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagus_river" title="Tagus river">Tagus river</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisboa" title="Lisboa">Lisboa</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nao_Victoria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nao_Victoria.jpg/250px-Nao_Victoria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nao_Victoria.jpg/375px-Nao_Victoria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nao_Victoria.jpg/500px-Nao_Victoria.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nao_Victoria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nao_Victoria" title="Nao Victoria">Nao Victoria</a></i> was the Spanish ship that managed to carry out the first <a href="/wiki/Circumnavigation" title="Circumnavigation">circumnavigation</a> in history. The present image shows a replica of <i>Victoria</i>, built in 1992, visiting <a href="/wiki/Nagoya" title="Nagoya">Nagoya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Expo_2005" title="Expo 2005">Expo 2005</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Age of Discovery</b> (or the <b>Age of Exploration</b>), also known as the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>, was a period largely overlapping with the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, approximately from the <a href="/wiki/15th_century" title="15th century">15th century</a> to the <a href="/wiki/17th_century" title="17th century">17th century</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">European history</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Seamanship" title="Seamanship">seafaring</a> Europeans explored regions across the globe.
</p><p>The extensive overseas exploration, with the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a> at the forefront, later joined by the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_overseas_possessions" title="English overseas possessions">English</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French</a>, emerged as a powerful factor in European culture, most notably the <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European encounter and colonization of the Americas</a>. It also marks an increased adoption of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> as a government policy in several European states. As such, it is sometimes synonymous with the <a href="/wiki/First_wave_of_European_colonization" title="First wave of European colonization">first wave of European colonization</a>.
</p><p>European exploration outside the Mediterranean started with the maritime expeditions of Portugal to the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> in 1336,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and later with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese discoveries</a> of the Atlantic archipelagos of <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>, the coast of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> in 1434 and the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_discovery_of_the_sea_route_to_India" title="Portuguese discovery of the sea route to India">establishment of the sea route to India</a> in 1498 by <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>, which is often considered a very remarkable voyage, as it initiated the Portuguese maritime and trade presence in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tapan_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tapan-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>A main event in the Age of Discovery took place when <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> sponsored the transatlantic <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">voyages of Christopher Columbus</a> between 1492 and 1504, which saw the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">colonization of the Americas</a>. Years later, the Spanish expedition of <a href="/wiki/Magellan_expedition" title="Magellan expedition">Magellan–Elcano expedition</a> made the first <a href="/wiki/Circumnavigation" title="Circumnavigation">circumnavigation</a> of the globe between 1519 and 1522, which was regarded as a major achievement in seamanship, and had a significant impact on the European understanding of the world. These discoveries led to numerous naval expeditions across the Atlantic, Indian, and Pacific oceans, and land expeditions in the Americas, Asia, Africa, and Australia that continued into the late 19th century, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Polar_exploration" title="Polar exploration">exploration of the polar regions</a> in the 20th century.
</p><p>European overseas exploration led to the rise of <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> and the European <a href="/wiki/Colonial_empire" title="Colonial empire">colonial empires</a>, with the contact between the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> (Europe, Asia, and Africa) and the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> (the Americas), as well as Australia, producing the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>, a wide transfer of plants, animals, food, human populations (including <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slaves</a>), <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">communicable diseases</a>, and culture between the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemispheres</a>. The Age of Discovery and later <a href="/wiki/Major_explorations_after_the_Age_of_Discovery" title="Major explorations after the Age of Discovery">European exploration</a> allowed the <a href="/wiki/History_of_cartography" title="History of cartography">mapping of the world</a>, resulting in a new worldview and distant civilizations coming into contact. At the same time, new diseases were propagated, decimating populations not previously in contact with the Old World, particularly <a href="/wiki/Native_American_disease_and_epidemics" title="Native American disease and epidemics">concerning Native Americans</a>. The era saw the widespread enslavement, exploitation and military conquest of native populations concurrent with the growing economic influence and spread of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">European culture</a> and technology.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept">Concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_(observation)" title="Discovery (observation)">Discovery (observation)</a></div>
<p>The concept of discovery has been scrutinized, critically highlighting the history of the core term of this <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washburn_1962_p=1_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Washburn_1962_p=1-4">[4]</a></sup> The term "age of discovery" has been in the historical literature and still commonly used. <a href="/wiki/J._H._Parry" title="J. H. Parry">J. H. Parry</a>, calling the period alternatively the <b>Age of Reconnaissance</b>, argues that not only was the era one of European explorations to regions heretofore unknown to them but that it also produced the expansion of geographical knowledge and empirical science. "It saw also the first major victories of empirical inquiry over authority, the beginnings of that close association of science, technology, and everyday work which is an essential characteristic of the modern western world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Pagden" title="Anthony Pagden">Anthony Pagden</a> draws on the work of <a href="/wiki/Edmundo_O%27Gorman" title="Edmundo O'Gorman">Edmundo O'Gorman</a> for the statement that "For all Europeans, the events of October 1492 constituted a 'discovery'. Something of which they had no prior knowledge had suddenly presented itself to their gaze."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagden19935_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagden19935-6">[6]</a></sup> O'Gorman argues further that the physical and geographical encounter with new territories was less important than the Europeans’ effort to integrate this new knowledge into their worldview, what he calls "the invention of America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Pagden examines the origins of the terms "discovery" and "invention". In English, "discovery" and its forms in the romance languages derive from "<i>disco-operio</i>, meaning to uncover, to reveal, to expose to the gaze” with the implicit idea that what was revealed existed previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagden19935–6_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagden19935–6-8">[8]</a></sup> Few Europeans during the period of explorations used the term "invention" for the European encounters, with the notable exception of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Waldseem%C3%BCller_map" title="Waldseemüller map">map</a> first used <a href="/wiki/Naming_of_the_Americas" title="Naming of the Americas">the term "America"</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagden19936_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagden19936-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>A central legal concept of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_Doctrine" title="Discovery Doctrine">Discovery Doctrine</a>, expounded by the United States Supreme Court in 1823, draws on assertions of European powers' right to claim land during their explorations.  The concept of "discovery" been used to enforce colonial claiming and the age of discovery, but has been also vocally challenged by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Assembly_of_First_Nations_2018_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Assembly_of_First_Nations_2018-10">[10]</a></sup> and researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frichner_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-frichner-11">[11]</a></sup> Many indigenous peoples have fundamentally challenged the concept and colonial claiming of "discovery" over their lands and people as forced and negating indigenous presence.
</p><p>The period alternatively called the <i>Age of Exploration</i>, has also been scrutinized through reflections on the understanding and use of <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">exploration</a>. Its understanding and use, like science more generally, has been discussed as being framed and used for colonial ventures, discrimination and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploitation_colonialism" title="Exploitation colonialism">exploitation</a>, by combining it with concepts such as the "<a href="/wiki/Frontier" title="Frontier">frontier</a>" (as in <a href="/wiki/Frontier_thesis" title="Frontier thesis">frontierism</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Manifest_destiny" title="Manifest destiny">manifest destiny</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_Conversation_2018_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Conversation_2018-12">[12]</a></sup> up to the contemporary age of <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renstrom_2021_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renstrom_2021-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, the term and concept of contact, as in <a href="/wiki/First_contact_(anthropology)" title="First contact (anthropology)">first contact</a>, has been used to shed a more nuanced and reciprocal light on the age of discovery and colonialism, using the alternative names of <b>Age of Contact</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> or <b>Contact Period</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nassaney_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nassaney-18">[18]</a></sup> discussing it as an "unfinished, diverse project".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silliman_2005_pp._55–74_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silliman_2005_pp._55–74-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_1999_p._20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson_1999_p.-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Portuguese began systematically exploring the Atlantic coast of Africa in 1418, under the sponsorship of Infante Dom Henrique (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">Prince Henry</a>). Under the direction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">Henry the Navigator</a>, the Portuguese developed a new, much lighter ship, the <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a>, which could sail farther and faster,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and, above all, was highly maneuverable and could sail much nearer the wind, or <i>into the wind</i>. In 1488, <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> reached the Indian Ocean by this route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>
In 1492, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Aragon</a> funded <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoese</a> mariner <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>'s plan to sail west to reach the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">Indies</a> by crossing the Atlantic. Columbus encountered a continent uncharted by most Europeans (though it had begun to be explored and <a href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_North_America" title="Norse colonization of North America">was temporarily colonized by the Norse</a> starting some 500 years earlier).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Later, it was called America after <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a>, a trader working for <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Portugal quickly claimed those lands under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alc%C3%A1%C3%A7ovas" title="Treaty of Alcáçovas">Treaty of Alcáçovas</a> but Castile was able to persuade the Pope, who was himself a Castilian, to issue <a href="/wiki/Bulls_of_Donation" title="Bulls of Donation">four papal bulls</a> to divide the world into two regions of exploration, where each kingdom had exclusive rights to claim newly discovered lands. These were modified by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a>, ratified by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:311px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="1056" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png/309px-Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png/464px-Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png/618px-Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png 2x" width="309"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Age_of_Discovery_explorations_in_English.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map with the main travels of the Age of Discovery. See details in an expandable table: <table class="wikitable sortable collapsible collapsed"> <caption>Major discoveries </caption> <tbody><tr> <th>Major discovery/<br/>Destination</th> <th>Main explorer</th> <th>Year</th> <th>Funding by </th></tr> <tr> <td><a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a></td> <td><a href="/wiki/Diogo_C%C3%A3o" title="Diogo Cão">Diogo </td></tr> <tr> <td><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a></td> <td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasco_Balboa" title="Vasco Balboa">Vasco Balboa</a></td> <td>1513</td> <td><a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Magellan" title="Strait of class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_East_India_Company" title="United East India Company">United East<br/> India Company</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a></td> <td><a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a></td> <td>1642</td> <td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_East_India_Company" title="United East India Company">United East<br/> India Company</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td>Islands Near <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a></td> <td><a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a></td> <td>1773</td> <td><a href="/wiki/George_III" title="George III">George III</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a></td> <td><a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a></td> <td>1778</td> <td><a href="/wiki/George_III" title="George III">George III</a> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>
<p>In 1498, a Portuguese expedition commanded by <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> reached India by sailing around Africa, opening up direct trade with Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> While other exploratory fleets were sent from Portugal to northern North America, in the following years <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India_Armadas" title="Portuguese India Armadas">Portuguese India Armadas</a> also extended this Eastern oceanic route, touching sometimes South America and by this way opening a circuit from the New World to Asia (starting in 1500, under the command of <a href="/wiki/Pedro_%C3%81lvares_Cabral" title="Pedro Álvares Cabral">Pedro Álvares Cabral</a>), and explored islands in the South Atlantic and Southern Indian Oceans. Soon, the Portuguese sailed further eastward, to the valuable <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Spice Islands</a> in 1512, landing in China one year later. <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">Japan was reached</a> by the Portuguese only in 1543. In 1513, Spanish <a href="/wiki/Vasco_N%C3%BA%C3%B1ez_de_Balboa" title="Vasco Núñez de Balboa">Vasco Núñez de Balboa</a> crossed the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a> and reached the "other sea" from the New World. Thus, Europe first received news of the eastern and western Pacific within a one-year span around 1512. East and west exploration overlapped in 1522, when a Castilian (Spanish) expedition, led by Portuguese navigator <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">Ferdinand Magellan</a> and, after his death in the <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku islands</a>, by Spanish Basque navigator <a href="/wiki/Juan_Sebasti%C3%A1n_Elcano" title="Juan Sebastián Elcano">Juan Sebastián Elcano</a>, sailing westward, completed the first circumnavigation of the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> while Spanish <i><a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">conquistadors</a></i> explored the interior of the Americas, and later, some of the South Pacific islands. The main objective of this voyage was to disrupt Portuguese trade in the East. 
</p><p>Since 1495, the French, the English, and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch</a> entered the race of exploration after learning of these exploits, defying the Iberian monopoly on maritime trade by searching for new routes, first to the western coasts of North and South America, through the first English and French expeditions (starting with the first expedition of <a href="/wiki/John_Cabot" title="John Cabot">John Cabot</a> in 1497 to the north, in the service of England, followed by the French expeditions to South America and later to North America), and into the Pacific Ocean around South America, but eventually by following the Portuguese around Africa into the Indian Ocean; discovering Australia in 1606, New Zealand in 1642, and Hawaii in 1778. Meanwhile, from the 1580s to the 1640s, Russians explored and conquered almost the whole of <a href="/wiki/Russian_conquest_of_Siberia" title="Russian conquest of Siberia">Siberia</a> and Alaska in the 1730s.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_world_maps" title="Early world maps">Early world maps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_European_exploration_of_Asia" title="Chronology of European exploration of Asia">Chronology of European exploration of Asia</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_European_trade">Rise of European trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rise of European trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_Rome" title="Fall of Rome">fall of Rome</a> largely severed the connection between Europe and lands further east, Christian Europe was largely a backwater compared to the Arab world, which quickly conquered and incorporated large territories in the Middle East and North Africa. The Christian crusades to retake the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> from the Muslims was not a military success, but it did bring Europe into contact with the Middle East and the valuable goods manufactured or traded there. From the 12th century, the European economy was transformed by the interconnecting of river and sea trade routes, leading Europe to create trading networks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 345">: 345 </span></sup>
</p><p>Before the 12th century, a major obstacle to trade east of the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>, which divided the Mediterranean Sea from the Atlantic Ocean, was Muslim control of great swaths of territory, including the Iberian peninsula and the trade monopolies of Christian city-states on the Italian peninsula, especially <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>. Economic growth of Iberia followed the <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Christian reconquest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> in what is now southern Spain and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Lisbon" title="Siege of Lisbon">Siege of Lisbon</a> (1147 AD), in Portugal. The decline of <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> naval strength that started before the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> helped the maritime Italian states, mainly Venice, Genoa and Pisa, dominate trade in the eastern Mediterranean, with merchants there becoming wealthy and politically influential. Further changing the mercantile situation in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a> was the waning of Christian Byzantine naval power following the death of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a> in 1180, whose dynasty had made several notable treaties and concessions with Italian traders, permitting the use of Byzantine Christian ports.  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest_of_England" title="Norman Conquest of England">Norman Conquest of England</a> in the late 11th century allowed for peaceful trade on the North Sea. The <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, a confederation of merchant guilds and their towns in northern Germany along the North Sea and Baltic Sea, was instrumental in commercial development of the region.  In the 12th century, the region of <a href="/wiki/County_of_Flanders" title="County of Flanders">Flanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_of_Hainaut" title="County of Hainaut">Hainault</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brabant" title="Duchy of Brabant">Brabant</a> produced the finest quality textiles in northern Europe, which encouraged merchants from Genoa and Venice to sail there directly from the Mediterranean through the Strait of Gibraltar and up the Atlantic coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 316–38">: 316–38 </span></sup> Nicolòzzo Spinola made the first recorded direct voyage from <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> to Flanders in 1277.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 328">: 328 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology:_Ship_Design_and_the_Compass">Technology: Ship Design and the Compass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Technology: Ship Design and the Compass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sd11-boom.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="1031" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Sd11-boom.JPG/220px-Sd11-boom.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Sd11-boom.JPG/330px-Sd11-boom.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Sd11-boom.JPG/440px-Sd11-boom.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sd11-boom.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A caravel with two lateen sail rigs and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headsail" title="Headsail">headsail</a>. Caravels were invented by the Portuguese, they had more manoeuvrability and were essential to the Age of Exploration.</div></div></div><p>Technological advancements that were important to the Age of Exploration were the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">magnetic compass</a> and advances in ship design.
</p><p>The compass was an addition to the ancient method of navigation based on sightings of the sun and stars.  The compass had been used for navigation in China by the 11th century and was adopted by the Arab traders in the Indian Ocean. The compass spread to Europe by the late 12th or early 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merson_1990_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merson_1990-31">[31]</a></sup>  Use of the compass for navigation in the Indian Ocean was first mentioned in 1232.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 351–2">: 351–2 </span></sup> The first mention of use of the compass in Europe was in 1180.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 382">: 382 </span></sup>  The Europeans used a "dry" compass, with a needle on a pivot.  The compass card was also a European invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese</a> built ocean going merchant ships called <i><a href="/wiki/K%27un-lun_po" title="K'un-lun po">po</a></i> since at least the 1st century AD. It was over 50 m in length and had a <a href="/wiki/Freeboard_(nautical)" title="Freeboard (nautical)">freeboard</a> of 5.2–7.8 m. The <i>po</i> was capable of carrying 700 people together with more than 10,000 <i>hú</i> (斛) of cargo (250–1000 tons according to various interpretations). They are built with multiple planks to resist storms, and had 4 sails plus a <a href="/wiki/Spritsail_(square-rigged)" title="Spritsail (square-rigged)">bowsprit sail</a>. The Javanese already reached <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana</a> by the 8th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 275–276">: 275–276 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 262">: 262 </span></sup>
</p><p>Ships grew in size, required smaller crews and were able to sail longer distances without stopping. This led to significant lower long-distance shipping costs by the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 342">: 342 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">Cogs</a> remained popular for trade because of their low cost. <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">Galleys</a> were also used in trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-7"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_geographical_knowledge_and_maps">Early geographical knowledge and maps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early geographical knowledge and maps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2108" data-file-width="1947" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg/170px-Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg/255px-Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg/340px-Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck._Model_ship_05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pintle" title="Pintle">Pintle</a>-and-<a href="/wiki/Gudgeon" title="Gudgeon">gudgeon</a> stern-post rudder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanseatic_league" title="Hanseatic league">Hanseatic league</a> flagship <i><a href="/wiki/Adler_von_L%C3%BCbeck" title="Adler von Lübeck">Adler von Lübeck</a></i> (1567–1581).</div></div></div><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i>, a document dating from 40 to 60 AD, describes a newly discovered route through the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, with descriptions of the markets in towns around Red Sea, <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and the Indian Ocean, including along the eastern coast of Africa, which states "for beyond these places the unexplored ocean curves around toward the west, and running along by the regions to the south of Aethiopia and Libya and Africa, it mingles with the western sea (possible reference to the Atlantic Ocean)".  European medieval knowledge about Asia beyond the reach of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> was sourced in partial reports, often obscured by legends,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> dating back from the time of the conquests of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> and his successors.
</p><p>Another source was the <a href="/wiki/Radhanite" title="Radhanite">Radhanite Jewish trade networks</a> of merchants established as go-betweens between Europe and the Muslim world during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">Crusader states</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1542" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg/220px-Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg/330px-Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg/440px-Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_world_map" title="Ptolemy's world map">Ptolemy's world map</a> (2nd century) in a 15th-century reconstruction by <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Germanus" title="Nicolaus Germanus">Nicolaus Germanus</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1154, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography in medieval Islam">Arab geographer</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Idrisi" title="Muhammad al-Idrisi">Muhammad al-Idrisi</a> created a description of the world and a <a href="/wiki/World_map" title="World map">world map</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tabula_Rogeriana" title="Tabula Rogeriana">Tabula Rogeriana</a>, at the court of King <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II of Sicily</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-houben_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-houben-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harley_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-harley-37">[37]</a></sup> but still Africa was only partially known to either Christians, Genoese and Venetians, or the Arab seamen, and its southern extent unknown. There were reports of great African <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>, but the factual knowledge was limited for the Europeans to the Mediterranean coasts and little else since the Arab blockade of North Africa precluded exploration inland. Knowledge about the Atlantic African coast was fragmented and derived mainly from <a href="/wiki/Early_world_maps" title="Early world maps">old</a> Greek and Roman maps based on Carthaginian knowledge, including the time of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> exploration of <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> was barely known and only trade links with the <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">Maritime republics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> especially, fostered collection of accurate maritime knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indian Ocean trade routes were sailed by Arab traders. Between 1405 and 1421, the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming China</a> sponsored a series of long range <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">tributary missions</a> under the command of <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> (Cheng Ho).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-39">[39]</a></sup> The fleets visited <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. But the journeys, reported by <a href="/wiki/Ma_Huan" title="Ma Huan">Ma Huan</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> voyager and translator, were halted abruptly after the emperor's death<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancall_2006_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mancall_2006-40">[40]</a></sup> and were not followed up, as the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> retreated in the <i><a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">haijin</a></i>, a policy of <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a>, having limited maritime trade.
</p><p>By 1400, a Latin translation of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographia" title="Geographia">Geographia</a></i> reached Italy coming from Constantinople. The rediscovery of Roman geographical knowledge was a revelation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> both for map-making and worldview,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> although reinforcing the idea that the Indian Ocean was landlocked.
</p>
<h3><span id="Medieval_European_travel_.281241.E2.80.931438.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_European_travel_(1241–1438)">Medieval European travel (1241–1438)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Medieval European travel (1241–1438)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2868" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/200px-Silk_route.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/300px-Silk_route.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/400px-Silk_route.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> routes later blocked by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1453 spurring exploration to find alternative sea routes</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3312" data-file-width="3654" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png/200px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png/300px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png/400px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> travels (1271–1295)</div></div></div>
<p>A prelude to the Age of Discovery was a series of European expeditions crossing <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> by land in the late Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> had threatened Europe, but Mongol states also unified much of Eurasia and, from 1206 on, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a></i> allowed safe trade routes and communication lines stretching from the Middle East to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A series of Europeans took advantage of these to explore eastwards. Most were Italians, as trade between Europe and the Middle East was controlled mainly by the <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">Maritime republics</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The close <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">Italian</a> links to the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> raised great curiosity and commercial interest in countries which lay further east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There are a few accounts of merchants from North Africa and the Mediterranean region who traded in the Indian Ocean in late medieval times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paine_2013_30-8"><a href="#cite_note-Paine_2013-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Christian embassies were sent as far as <a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">Karakorum</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_the_Levant" title="Mongol invasions of the Levant">Mongol invasions of the Levant</a>, from which they gained a greater understanding of the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The first of these travellers was <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Pian_del_Carpine" title="Giovanni da Pian del Carpine">Giovanni da Pian del Carpine</a>, dispatched by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Khan" title="Great Khan">Great Khan</a>, who journeyed to <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and back from 1241 to 1247.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-44">[44]</a></sup> About the same time, Russian prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaroslav_of_Vladimir" title="Yaroslav of Vladimir">Yaroslav of Vladimir</a>, and subsequently his sons <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nevsky" title="Alexander Nevsky">Alexander Nevsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrey_II_of_Vladimir" title="Andrey II of Vladimir">Andrey II of Vladimir</a>, travelled to the Mongolian capital. Though having strong political implications, their journeys left no detailed accounts. Other travellers followed, like French <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_de_Longjumeau" title="André de Longjumeau">André de Longjumeau</a> and Flemish <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">William of Rubruck</a>, who reached China through Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, a Venetian merchant, dictated an account of journeys throughout Asia from 1271 to 1295, describing being a guest at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a> court of <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">Travels</a></i>, and it was read throughout Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Muslim fleet guarding the Strait of Gibraltar was defeated by Genoa in 1291.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In that year, the Genoese attempted their first Atlantic exploration attempt when merchant brothers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vadino_and_Ugolino_Vivaldi" title="Vadino and Ugolino Vivaldi">Vadino and Ugolino Vivaldi</a> sailed from Genoa with two galleys but disappeared off the Moroccan coast, feeding the fears of oceanic travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> From 1325 to 1354, a <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Moroccan</a> scholar from <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">Tangier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a>, journeyed through North Africa, the Sahara desert, West Africa, Southern Europe, Eastern Europe, the Horn of Africa, the Middle East and Asia, having reached China. After returning, he dictated an account of his journeys to a scholar he met in Granada, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rihla" title="The Rihla">The Rihla</a></i> ("The Journey"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> the unheralded source on his adventures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Between 1357 and 1371 a book of supposed travels compiled by <a href="/wiki/John_Mandeville" title="John Mandeville">John Mandeville</a> acquired extraordinary popularity. Despite the unreliable and often fantastical nature of its accounts it was used as a reference<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> for the East, Egypt, and the Levant in general, asserting the old belief that Jerusalem was the <a href="/wiki/Axis_mundi" title="Axis mundi">centre of the world</a>.
</p><p>Following the period of <a href="/wiki/Timurid_relations_with_Europe" title="Timurid relations with Europe">Timurid relations with Europe</a>, in 1439 <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_de%27_Conti" title="Niccolò de' Conti">Niccolò de' Conti</a> published an account of his travels as a Muslim merchant to India and Southeast Asia and, later in 1466–1472, Russian merchant <a href="/wiki/Afanasy_Nikitin" title="Afanasy Nikitin">Afanasy Nikitin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tver" title="Tver">Tver</a> travelled to India, which he described in his book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Journey_Beyond_the_Three_Seas" title="A Journey Beyond the Three Seas">A Journey Beyond the Three Seas</a></i>.
</p><p>These overland journeys had little immediate effect. The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> collapsed almost as quickly as it formed and soon the route to the east became more difficult and dangerous. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> of the 14th century also blocked travel and trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> further limited the possibilities of European overland trade.
</p>
<h3><span id="Chinese_missions_.281405.E2.80.931433.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_missions_(1405–1433)">Chinese missions (1405–1433)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Chinese missions (1405–1433)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">Ming treasure voyages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_exploration" title="Chinese exploration">Chinese exploration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="1385" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg/150px-Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg/225px-Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg/300px-Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stellardiagram-Zhengho.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Mao Kun map", believed to be based on <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>'s travels, showing sailing directions between ports of SE Asia and as far as Malindi, in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_Bei_Zhi" title="Wu Bei Zhi">Wu Bei Zhi</a></i> (1628)</div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese had wide connections through trade in Asia and had been sailing to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> (AD 618–907). Between 1405 and 1421, the third Ming emperor <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle</a> sponsored a series of long range <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tributary missions</a> in the Indian Ocean under the command of admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> (Cheng Ho).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-39">[39]</a></sup> As important as they are, these voyages did not result in permanent links to overseas territories because of isolationist policy changes in China ending the voyages and knowledge of them.
</p><p>A large fleet of new <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junk</a> ships was prepared for these international diplomatic expeditions. The largest of these junks—that the Chinese termed <a href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ship" title="Chinese treasure ship"><i>bao chuan</i> (treasure ships)</a>—may have measured 121 metres (400 feet) stem to stern, and thousands of sailors were involved. The first expedition departed in 1405. At least seven well-documented expeditions were launched, each bigger and more expensive than the last. The fleets visited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">Malay Archipelago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> (at the time called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siam" title="Siam">Siam</a>), exchanging goods along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> They presented gifts of gold, silver, <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>; in return, received such novelties as <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostriches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">giraffes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> After the emperor's death, Zheng He led a final expedition departing from Nanking in 1431 and returning to Beijing in 1433. It is very likely that this last expedition reached as far as <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>. The travels were reported by <a href="/wiki/Ma_Huan" title="Ma Huan">Ma Huan</a>, a Muslim voyager and translator who accompanied Zheng He on three of the seven expeditions, his account published as the <i><a href="/wiki/Yingya_Shenglan" title="Yingya Shenglan">Yingya Shenglan</a></i> (Overall Survey of the Ocean's Shores) (1433).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The voyages had a significant and lasting effect on the organization of a <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">maritime network</a>, utilizing and creating nodes and conduits in its wake, thereby restructuring international and cross-cultural relationships and exchanges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-60">[60]</a></sup> It was especially impactful as no other polity had exerted naval dominance over all sectors of the Indian Ocean prior to these voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The Ming promoted alternative nodes as a strategy to establish control over the network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-62">[62]</a></sup> For instance, due to Chinese involvement, ports such as <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a> (in Southeast Asia), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cochin" title="Cochin">Cochin</a> (on the Malabar Coast), and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> (on the Swahili Coast) had grown as key alternatives to other important and established ports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> The appearance of the Ming treasure fleet generated and intensified competition among contending polities and rivals, each seeking an alliance with the Ming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The voyages also brought about the Western Ocean's <a href="/wiki/Regional_integration" title="Regional integration">regional integration</a> and the increase in <a href="/wiki/Mobilities" title="Mobilities">international circulation</a> of people, ideas, and goods. It also provided a platform for <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitanism" title="Cosmopolitanism">cosmopolitan</a> discourses, which took place in locations such as the ships of the Ming treasure fleet, the Ming capitals of Nanjing as well as Beijing, and the banquet receptions organized by the Ming court for foreign representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-60">[60]</a></sup> Diverse groups of people from across the maritime countries congregated, interacted, and traveled together as the Ming treasure fleet sailed from and to Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-60">[60]</a></sup> For the first time in its history, the maritime region from China to Africa was under the dominance of a single imperial power and thereby allowed for the creation of a cosmopolitan space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>These long-distance journeys were not followed up, as the Chinese Ming dynasty retreated in the <i><a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">haijin</a></i>, a policy of <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a>, having limited maritime trade. Travels were halted abruptly after the emperor's death, as the Chinese lost interest in what they termed barbarian lands, turning inward,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancall_2006_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mancall_2006-40">[40]</a></sup> and successor emperors felt the expeditions were harmful to the Chinese state; <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> ended further expeditions and <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> suppressed much of the information about Zheng He's voyages.
</p>
<h2><span id="Atlantic_Ocean_.281419.E2.80.931507.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_Ocean_(1419–1507)">Atlantic Ocean (1419–1507)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Atlantic Ocean (1419–1507)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history_of_Europe#The_European_Age_of_Discovery_(1400–1600)" title="Maritime history of Europe">Maritime history of Europe § The European Age of Discovery (1400–1600)</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese discoveries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="943" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/220px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/330px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/440px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> (red) and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venetian</a> (green) maritime trade routes in the Mediterranean and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a></div></div></div>
<p>From the 8th century until the 15th century, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> and neighboring <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">maritime republics</a> held the monopoly of European trade with the Middle East. The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">silk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a>, involving spices, <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a>, herbs, drugs and <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>, made these Mediterranean city-states phenomenally rich. Spices were among the most expensive and demanded products of the Middle Ages, as they were used in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_medicine_of_Western_Europe" title="Medieval medicine of Western Europe">medieval medicine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> religious rituals, cosmetics, perfumery, as well as food additives and preservatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> They were all imported from Asia and Africa.
</p><p>Muslim traders—mainly descendants of Arab sailors from <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>—dominated maritime routes throughout the Indian Ocean, tapping source regions in the Far East and shipping for trading emporiums in India, mainly <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">Kozhikode</a>, westward to <a href="/wiki/Ormus" title="Ormus">Ormus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeddah" title="Jeddah">Jeddah</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. From there, overland routes led to the Mediterranean coasts. Venetian merchants distributed the goods through Europe until the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, that eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453, barring Europeans from important combined-land-sea routes in areas around the Aegean, Bosporus, and Black Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GT-DEX-1453-09_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-GT-DEX-1453-09-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GT-DEX-1453-10_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-GT-DEX-1453-10-69">[68]</a></sup> The Venetians and other Italian Maritime Republics maintained some, more limited, access to Asian goods, via south-eastern Mediterranean trade, in such ports as Antioch, Acre, and Alexandria.
</p><p>Forced to reduce their activities in the Black Sea, and at war with Venice, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> had turned to North African trade of wheat, olive oil (valued also as an energy source) and a search for silver and gold. Europeans had a constant <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Revolution#Monetary_factors" title="Commercial Revolution">deficit in silver and gold</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> as coin only went one way: out, spent on eastern trade that was now cut off. Several European mines were exhausted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> the lack of <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a> leading to the development of a complex banking system to manage the risks in trade (the very first state bank, <i><a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Saint_George" title="Bank of Saint George">Banco di San Giorgio</a></i>, was founded in 1407 at Genoa). Sailing also into the ports of <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a> (Flanders) and England, Genoese communities were then established in Portugal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> who profited from their enterprise and financial expertise.
</p><p>European sailing had been primarily close to land <a href="/wiki/Cabotage" title="Cabotage">cabotage</a>, guided by <a href="/wiki/Portolan_chart" title="Portolan chart">portolan charts</a>. These charts specified proven ocean routes guided by coastal landmarks: sailors departed from a known point, followed a compass heading, and tried to identify their location by its landmarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> For the first oceanic exploration Western Europeans used the compass, as well as progressive new advances in <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> and astronomy. Arab navigational tools like the <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(instrument)" title="Quadrant (instrument)">quadrant</a> were used for <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_exploration">Portuguese exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Portuguese exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_maritime_exploration" title="Portuguese maritime exploration">Portuguese maritime exploration</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European exploration of Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG/220px-Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG/330px-Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG/440px-Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Saharan trade routes</a> c. 1400, with modern <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> highlighted</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg"><img alt="A map of Africa as it was known to Europeans in the 15th century. created by German cartographer Lienhart Hol and based on Ptolemey's fourth map of Africa" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6861" data-file-width="9360" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg/220px-Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg/330px-Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg/440px-Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quarta_et_Ultima_Affrice_Tabula_NLI.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> as it was known to Europeans in 1482. created by German cartographer Lienhart Holl and based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemey" title="Ptolemey">Ptolemey</a>'s fourth map of Africa</div></div></div>
<p>In 1297, King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinis_of_Portugal" title="Dinis of Portugal">Dinis of Portugal</a> took personal interest in exports. In 1317, he made an agreement with Genoese merchant sailor <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Pessanha" title="Manuel Pessanha">Manuel Pessanha</a> (Pessagno), appointing him first <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">admiral</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Navy" title="Portuguese Navy">Portuguese Navy</a>, with the goal of defending the country against Muslim pirate raids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> Outbreaks of <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> led to severe depopulation in the second half of the 14th century: only the sea offered alternatives, with most population settling in fishing and trading coastal areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Between 1325 and 1357, <a href="/wiki/Afonso_IV_of_Portugal" title="Afonso IV of Portugal">Afonso IV of Portugal</a> encouraged maritime commerce and ordered the first explorations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, already known to the Genoese, were claimed as officially discovered under patronage of the Portuguese but in 1344 Castile disputed them, expanding their rivalry into the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>To ensure their monopoly on trade, Europeans (beginning with the Portuguese) attempted to install a mediterranean system of trade which used military might and intimidation to divert trade through ports they controlled; there it could be taxed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> In 1415, <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">Ceuta</a> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Ceuta" title="Conquest of Ceuta">conquered</a> by the Portuguese aiming to control navigation of the African coast. Young prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">Henry the Navigator</a> was there and became aware of profit possibilities in the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trade</a> routes. For centuries <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_slave_trade" title="Trans-Saharan slave trade">slave and gold</a> trade routes linking West Africa with the Mediterranean passed over the Western Sahara Desert, controlled by the Moors of North Africa.
</p><p>Henry wished to know how far Muslim territories in Africa extended, hoping to bypass them and trade directly with West Africa by sea, find allies in legendary Christian lands to the south<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> like the long-lost Christian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Prester_John" title="Prester John">Prester John</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> and to probe whether it was possible to reach the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">Indies</a> by sea, the source of the lucrative <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a>. He invested in sponsoring voyages down the coast of <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, gathering a group of merchants, shipowners and stakeholders interested in new sea lanes. Soon the Atlantic islands of <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> (1419) and the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> (1427) were reached. In particular, they were discovered by voyages launched by the command of Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">Henry the Navigator</a>. The expedition leader himself, who established settlements on the island of Madeira, was Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Gon%C3%A7alves_Zarco" title="João Gonçalves Zarco">João Gonçalves Zarco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time, Europeans did not know what lay beyond <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Non" title="Cape Non">Cape Non</a> (Cape Chaunar) on the African coast, and whether it was possible to return once it was crossed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Nautical myths warned of oceanic monsters or an edge of the world, but Prince Henry's navigation challenged such beliefs: starting in 1421, systematic sailing overcame it, reaching the difficult <a href="/wiki/Cape_Bojador" title="Cape Bojador">Cape Bojador</a> that in 1434 one of Prince Henry's captains, <a href="/wiki/Gil_Eanes" title="Gil Eanes">Gil Eanes</a>, finally passed.
</p><p>A major advance was the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a> in the mid-15th century, a small ship able to sail <a href="/wiki/Windward_and_leeward" title="Windward and leeward">windward</a> more than any other in Europe at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Evolved from fishing ships designs, they were the first that could leave the coastal <a href="/wiki/Cabotage" title="Cabotage">cabotage</a> navigation and sail safely on the open Atlantic. For <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a> the Portuguese used the <a href="/wiki/Ephemeris" title="Ephemeris">ephemerides</a>, which experienced a remarkable diffusion in the 15th century. These were astronomical charts plotting the location of the stars over a distinct period of time. Published in 1496 by the Jewish astronomer, astrologer, and mathematician <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">Abraham Zacuto</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">Almanach</a> <a href="/wiki/Perpetuum" title="Perpetuum">Perpetuum</a> included some of these tables for the movements of stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> These tables revolutionized navigation, allowing the calculation of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>. Exact <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, however, remained elusive, and mariners struggled to determine it for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Using the caravel, systematic exploration continued ever more southerly, advancing on average one degree a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cap-Vert" title="Cap-Vert">Cape Verde Peninsula</a> were reached in 1445 and in 1446, <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Fernandes" title="Álvaro Fernandes">Álvaro Fernandes</a> pushed on almost as far as present-day <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>.
</p><p>In 1453, the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> to the hands of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottomans" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> was a blow to Christendom and the established business relations linking with the east. In 1455, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">Pope Nicholas V</a> issued the <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">bull</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Romanus_Pontifex" title="Romanus Pontifex">Romanus Pontifex</a></i> reinforcing the previous <i><a href="/wiki/Dum_Diversas" title="Dum Diversas">Dum Diversas</a></i> (1452), granting all lands and seas discovered beyond Cape Bojador to King <a href="/wiki/Afonso_V_of_Portugal" title="Afonso V of Portugal">Afonso V of Portugal</a> and his successors, as well as trade and conquest against Muslims and pagans, initiating a <i><a href="/wiki/Mare_clausum" title="Mare clausum">mare clausum</a></i> policy in the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> The king, who had been inquiring of Genoese experts about a seaway to India, commissioned the <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">Fra Mauro world map</a>, which arrived in Lisbon in 1459.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
